0446402397|4.0|I would have given this book five stars but for a coincidental ending (which I won't spoil by giving away), that struck me as pure Hollywood calling: 'Get all the characters to intertwine in some way.' It's probably no surprise then that Tom Rob Smith earned his writing spurs writing screenplays, and this book was snapped up early for a film treatment by no less than Ridley Scott.     What works the best in this book is the way in which everyone serves Stalin's party machine in some form: it permeates reactions to the boys' deaths (based on a true life series of murders), the overall investigation, and how work mates, relatives and partners interact.     Particularly moving was how the main character, state investigator Leo Demitov is unsure at one point whether to betray his wife in order to save his own and his parent's neck. His wife in turn lies to escape the fate he might decide for her, and both are left questioning whether any semblance of love was their mutual attraction, or was it just more state-position power-brokering: he was attracted to her because she was a teacher and intellectual which would look good to his superiors; and she was attracted to him because the power of his position might protect her.    In the end it's fear and survival which binds them - probably as close to love as can be clung to in the stifling state-controlled atmosphere portrayed in this gripping thriller. Put it high on your reading list.

She hates when they disappear for the summer. When she was eleven, she was attacked by those wolves, and one of them saved her. Ever since then, the yellow-eyed wolf has always been \\"her\\" wolf, and seemed to always watch over her. But then the wolves attack Jack Culpeper, who is the typical rich, snobbish jerk at school. She takes him to the ER, and it soon becomes apparent that he is her yellow-eyed wolf. The werewolves are wolves when it is cold and humans when it is warm, but each year they need more warmth to transform back into humans. Eventually they stop turning back, and this is probably Sam's final year to be human.<br /><br />The idea for the wolves being human when it is hot out was a great idea. They have some problems remembering stuff from their human lives, and vice versa. Their means of communications is sharing mental pictures. Instead, there are instincts that don't even allow Grace to get close to Sam.<br /><br />Grace was also bitten by the werewolves, but she has never transformed. It at first sounded like there was a quirk with her, almost like (again, Twilight, I'm sorry) Edward not being able to read Bella's mind. There's a possibility that it's not quite like that.<br /><br />The people seem mostly normal, for the most part. Grace's eccentric painter of a mother is always busy, and her father is always out. She has to fend for herself and manages to keep a guy in her house for days without them realizing. It's a little unrealistic, in my opinion, and also slightly overdone. Grace's friends are very normal together- they aren't always sure what to do, they have a couple fights between each other, and so on. Sometimes they are a little crazy and hyper. They need coffee. And so on. Sam and the pack are all a little different because of their different lifestyle. He grew up being homeschooled in the summer, when he was human, and most of his interactions were with other werewolves. Sam enjoys language and words because for a large part of his time, he can't use them, but he will make up little poems or song lyrics. I'm a bit of a poet myself, and unfortunately you can't just come up with good, rhyming lyrics off the top of your head like he seems to do. It takes a little bit of time to write the four lines with all of the right phrasing and sounds. One of the most minor details in the book that I didn't enjoy. Sam and Grace seem normal most of the time, but at times it becomes obvious that Grace is quiet and doesn't like a lot of people while Sam is almost a little too sensitive, bordering on emo or being a loner or something. I likethis quality about them- they seem relatable while still being themselves. The facts that the two of them have \\"known\\" each other for years and that Grace actually has friends and fits in somewhere are pluses in the book. There are just too many stories where the new girl meets the supernatural boy and they end up together.<br /><br />I enjoyed one part of the story where Sam is reading a bit of German poetry to Grace, because of how it sounds. While reading the translation, he breaks off and says that he doesn't like this translation. Because I know German, it was interesting because I know exactly what it meant. I feel like there would be a difference in how you take that scene and interpret it based on whether you know the language or not and if you understand the whole quote.<br /><br />I would have to say, try this book. If you liked Twilight, you'll enjoy this. If you didn't like Twilight because of the annoying characters, you'll enjoy this. If you thought the vampires were a little too tame in Twilight, you'll enjoy this. If you found Stephanie Meyer's lack of mentioning one specific topic that comes up in all too many YA and adult books childish and naive, you'll enjoy this (I'd say that this is on the same level as The Vampire Diaries TV series on that subject, so if you're ready for a book a little more mature than Twilight or have seen the series, you'll be fine). If you haven't read Twilight because you were told what a lousy book it was or didn't want to get caught up in the craze, you'll most likely enjoy this book. It takes a pretty significant even to finally get her to see another side, but it happens. Similar to what I said in my review of Wings by Aprilynn Pike, Lauren doesn't make everything happy and perfect from the start. Lena learns and grows throughout the book, and reading that kept me turning the page.<br /><br />I can't say the book was perfect. There were some areas where I felt it slowed down a little too much for me, but there was always enough to keep me going. Admittedly, I didn't know this was a trilogy and was originally disappointed with the ending. After some research (thank you Goodreads) I learned there are two more books, and that made me very happy! I'm looking forward to the next book  in this series (Pandemonium) and I intend to read Before I Fall.rom the start. At first she was all alone and wanted to die, but then Jack came along and decided to live.<br />  Jack is raised in a completely sterile environment; he seems to live in a fairytale world. A world created by his mother in order to protect him from the idea, or the dreams, of a freedom that is not about to come. His only connection to the outside world is the television; though he believes that everything he sees there is not true, apart from Dora, his very best friend. He also truly thinks that apart from himself, Dora, his mother and old Nick that there are no other people in the world. 0061452068|5.0|Regardless of what naysayers claim, the lot of humanity has improved steadily over the ages.  Longevity, poverty, education, illness - these and other indicators can be counted as improved.  Why?  According to Ridley, the most important factors are specialization and exchange, or basically the underpinnings of a capitalist economy.  Ridley shows how these two factors allowed man to increase his standard of living - the farmer could trade with the fisherman, who could trade with the weaver, and so on, allowing each of them to have more products at lower prices. Ridley emphasizes that lowered costs comes not just in nominal prices but also in the amount of time one needs to work to earn enough to buy goods and services.  Today in the US, the poorest have amenities that European royalty in the 18th cebtury never had - indoor lighting, plumbing, refrigeration, etc.  We take this for granted and forget how far we have come.  Is everything perfect?  No.  But Ridley asserts that the exchange of ideas and free economies will continue to provide improvements in all areas.  Ridley tackles the difficult task of challenging global warming doomsayers - he is not a denier but he gives a balanced presentation of its history as well as the potential harm from renewable energy, something that is never mentioned.  For example, he explains how much land would need to be used by certain biofuels, reducing the amount available for nature.  I felt good just reading the opening chapter because Ridley accurately describes how good we have it today and how much better it can continue to get, for all, if we don't forget the true sources of our proesprity.

0439023513|1.0|Before I get into my review, let me start out by saying that the romance has always been my favorite part of this series.  Yes, I realize that the books aren't really about that, but that's what I loved and like it or not, it has been a big part of the series. So most of my review will be about that.      SPOILERS AHEAD        Peeta and Katniss' relationship was my favorite parts of the first two books.  Their scenes together captured my imagination.  Even though Katniss was pretending in HG, there was a chemistry between them that I loved immediately. The ending to that book broke my heart, but then CF gave me renewed hope.  It was great seeing them bond together, and how miserable Katniss was when he was captured. Then this book happened.  At first, things were going well. I was excited, even if Katniss' seemed a little flat in this book.  I knew this was going to be the book that she finally told Peeta that she loved him - Gale had already got chosen in CF, while Peeta didn't have anything to show for his efforts yet, so I had a good feeling that this book was finally going to be the payoff to all that buildup.  My prediction was going well at first, it seemed the evidence was mounting that Katniss loved Peeta and that she was going to choose him (she was mourning the loss of a feeling she couldn't put a name to, kissing the pearl, when he was first rescued she wanted to be the first face he saw, and to kiss him again.) So I kept reading, eager for the inevitable \\"I love you\\" that I felt he had earned many times over and dreading that he would die immediately afterwards or reject it thanks to his hijacking... but that just added to the suspense. The stories were building to that, right? I was looking forward to that moment, so I kept reading through a book that I wasn't really otherwise enjoying (definitely not like the first two.)     And as I read, I started to get worried - Katniss had all but tossed up her hands and given up on him. What was going on? The moment of confession still hadn't come towards the end, and it was beginning to feel like it would never happen. I had been hoping it would come at some crucial point in the war, when Katniss would somehow see her love for Peeta and wouldn't be able to withhold it anymore, but no.  Maybe I was wrong?  But I was sure that I wasn't, it seemed like poor writing to have all that buildup and hints and then go nowhere with that plot.    It came at the very end. As an afterthought. A sad concession of love from one broken person to another. I was happy, but I also felt hollow. It wasn't what I had hoped for at all. It wasn't satisfying. I guess maybe it was realistic, but I didn't really want realism, at least not when it came to their relationship.  On a positive note, I did find the \\"Real or not real?\\" \\"Real.\\" very sweet and it did bring a tear to my eye...but I wish we had gotten to see more of their \\"growing back together.\\" The ending of the story reminded me of a summary instead of an actual part of the story - but more on that in a minute.    Thinking about it more, I think the biggest problem I have is that both Peeta and Katniss could have died many times over at the end, and Peeta would have never known that Katniss loved him. That seems so cold. When they were going to separate, I really expected that to be where she would say it. It makes me sad to think that after all Peeta had gone through for her sake, he could have easily died thinking that he was unloved, or Katniss could have died and he would have lived his entire life not knowing that the person he loved most in the world loved him back. I guess Katniss had a lot on her plate at the moment, but I wish she could have for once taken time to consider him and her feelings for him.  I didn't even want a \\"fairytale ending\\" for them, I just wanted the moment to have more of an impact.    Anyway, back to the point I was making before.  Besides the romance, I feel this book highlighted a problem with all three of the books (in varying degrees). Collins has a bad habit of telling, not showing.  The problem was horribly apparent in this book. Katniss tells us about her emotions, we don't really get to see them. In the beginning of the book I got the sense that she was really broken up about Peeta. But once Peeta shows back up, I never got a real sense of her emotions ever again. In fact, Peeta showing back up and strangling Katniss is a perfect example of what I'm talking about. I was shocked and excited by the end of that chapter and I left off because I thought the next chapter would be amazing.  But...we just pick up with Katniss waking up and we're told a bit about what had happened. How much better would it have been if we were with her in that scene, feeling her horror and confusion as someone who she had missed so much was strangling her?  It would have been so much better if we could have seen everyone else around her reacted, and then how she felt when she learned that he had been hijacked, but we're just told about it after the fact.      After that, Katniss tells us she's sad, and she also tells us she's annoyed. She says she accepts that Peeta is never coming back and makes out with Gale.  Okay, but how did she come to that conclusion?   How would a real person feel if someone who they cared about deeply was brainwashed and they were partially responsible for it? How would a real person feel about accepting that the person is never going to be the same again, that they're gone mentally forever? I imagine there would be more to it than \\"Oh that's sad. I'm sad. ...Well, might as well go with my next best option to console myself.\\" I'm not even saying that it's unrealistic to seek comfort from someone else, but her thought process and emotions left much to be desired.  She tells us she wants to kill Snow, she tells us she's sad about all the deaths she's witnessed but again, it was hard for me to get a sense of her actual emotions or get behind her motivation to kill Coin. It was hard for me to even be sad about Prim's death, which should have been tragic.     Katniss didn't feel like an actual person, she felt like a character that I was being told about by someone else who had read a book and was now describing it to me. And don't even get me started about the endless exposition about the war, especially at the end of the war, where Katniss fails her mission and then hears all the details from other people. (That sounds familiar!) It felt like an info dump honestly. Not at all the kind of action I was hoping for.  Then of course, there's the ending, where we're shown absolutely nothing.  We're just given a summary about how Katniss came back to life and grew together with Peeta and tried to move on.  I could have gotten the same information on Wikipedia and saved myself a lot of time and energy.

B004TRKH9O|5.0|My impression of Zeit is that the band members of Tangerine Dream were aiming to try and out-do the dark, nightmarish psychedelic period of Pink Floyd (such as what we experienced from albums like A Saucerful of Secrets, Meddle and even the studio portion of Ummagumma for that matter).  I think it actually works.  This is some EXTREMELY moving stuff.    Unlike Pink Floyd however, there's no actual songwriting to speak of.  Just waves upon waves and layers upon layers of incredibly spooky soundtrack/ambient sounds that will definitely take your mind through a variety of the creepiest settings and places in the entire universe.  Seriously, this stuff is so whacked out that your imagination won't stop at just planet Earth.  This journey takes us to Neptune and beyond!    Interesting side note- the more you hear this stuff, the more it absorbs into your mind.  The ambient sounds actually manage to come together to create lengthy masterpieces.  I can't explain HOW it works, but it does.  Give it a chance.  Definitely the kind of album you need to hear several times in order to fully and properly digest.  My favorite song by FAR is the opener titled \\"Birth of Liquid Plejades\\".  The first 7 minutes sounds like nothing but the DARKEST violin (or cello) solo in the history of mankind.  It sounds like it's just repeatedly playing one or two ominous notes over and over, totally reminiscent of the soundtrack from the relatively popular film There Will Be Blood.  After that portion of the song is over, these organs come into the picture and they're noticeably creepy as well.  The synths are remarkably melodic, if I recall.  That carries us through most of the song.  Let me describe \\"Birth of Liquid Plejades\\" even more.  The cellos actually remind me of this forest area that takes about 10 minutes to walk there from my house.  This area is right when the Amish fields stop and the woods appear.  It's pretty darn creepy, especially 20 minutes after the sun sets on a calm October night.  The woods sort of go around in a circle, and when the conditions are right I feel like I'm being haunted by something.  I've also seen some strange things back there such as weird bugs and animals I couldn't recognize coming out of the woods and looking at me.  I've also had pets buried back there.  This song makes me think about that particular area and really elevates the feeling of creepiness.  Now after those 7 minutes of cellos conclude, the song seems to take another ghostly turn- this time making me think about ghost ships that have disappeared into the fog and were never seen again.  This section of the song really captures the feeling of mystery concerning the ships and even includes these occasional ghostly hawls to really elevate the atmosphere.  These ghostly sounds always appear at JUST the right time.  I love it.  The song then finishes up with some organs to make it seem like everything we've just experienced is related to heaven in some way.  Just a wonderful song overall and one that's totally unlike anything else.    \\"Nebulous Dawn\\" is mostly made up of, again, distinctly creepy nightmare world settings using some of the most frightening ambient sounds in existence (or rather, the most frightening ambient sounds technologically available at the time).  Alright, these tricks were probably used in several horror or science fiction films back in the day, but nobody ever released an entire albums worth of similar effects, have they?  If they have, it surely wasn't 70 minutes long and probably wasn't NEARLY as terrifying as the ambient tricks presented here.    This is where Tangerine Dream deserves credit for Zeit- it's a daring experiment, and I say \\"daring\\" because there was a good chance this kind of stuff would have been immediately rejected by the general public back in the day had Tangerine Dream been as popular as Pink Floyd, and you know, perhaps it was rejected in some areas of the world.  I don't believe Tangerine Dream was popular enough for any rejection to be taken into consideration though (at least, not in America in the early 70's).    Anyway \\"Nebulous Dawn\\" feels like what would happen if bright lights were to shine through my bedroom window one night, and I was then forced to walk outside across a wheat field, through some thick weeds and eventually to these caves where aliens were planning to abduct me.  Incredibly atmospheric.  The weak point is probably \\"Origin of Supernatural Probabilities\\".  By \\"weak\\" I of course mean \\"Extremely cool but the worst song here\\".  The ambience is pretty good but there's a few moments when the atmosphere does nothing remotely interesting.  However when it *is* interesting (such as the first half with the clever use of ghostly shrills and other effects involving these tricks that continuously fade in and out when you pay extremely close attention to the piece- it's effectively breathtaking to say the least) you will be blown away completely.  Some of the ambient sounds remind me of a laboratory of sorts where viral experiments take place, and where bodies are stored or frozen for future studies.  Even a zombie setting wouldn't be out of the question.  Awesome.    Oh, and the title song.  PLEASE, listen to the first few minutes and tell me THAT doesn't remind you of an atmospheric world in a Metroid game (\\"Metroid\\" is a video game series in case you aren't familiar with the name).  The rest of the song has quietly performed ominous waves of unusual ambient sounds with a dangerously thick and intense atmosphere.  I admire it quite a bit.  Zeit may just be the craziest, creepiest, oddest, strangest, darkest soundtrack I've ever heard.  That is, if you were to call it a soundtrack.  It probably doesn't qualify as such, but it sure *sounds* like one.

B00005JKLZ|5.0|THE TIME MACHINE is a science fiction romance featuring Guy Pearce as the college professor and Samantha Mumba as the love interest.  Ms.Mumba lives in the distant future, and resides in a primitive vertical village built into the steep sides of a canyon.  The movie features plenty of gee-whiz gizmos.  These gizmos will be the delight, especially to children between the ages of eight to fourteen.  The opening sequence of the movie takes place in a college and in the professor's laboratory, and the viewer is treated to plenty of gizmos, including an early version of the electric toothbrush.  Then, we see the time machine, with its brass fittings and whirling crystalline components.  And then, in the distant future, the viewer is treated to a melange of shelters, ladders, windmills, and other devices, all of wood and natural fibers, which belong to the primitive civilization of the Eloi people.     In my opinion, the first third of the film is the best part.  Mr.Pearce is courting actress Sienna Guillory, in the spare moments that somehow seem to be left free from his busy schedule of teaching and research.  The turn-of-the-century society, as shown in the first third of the film, is one of turn-of-the-century prudishness.  In one scene, Ms.Guillory is shocked (but happily so) when Mr.Pearce kisses her in public.  While at an evening ice skating date, Mr. Pearce presents Ms.Guillory with an engagement ring.  Ms.Guillory is not only beautiful, but her lips and cheeks quivver in a fascinating and fetching manner, in response to Mr.Pearce's interest in marriage.  Any viewer of this display of little twitches and quivverings of pleasure, will be convinced that such is the stuff of movie actors (and not within the achievable realm of any ordinary amateur actor).      At any rate, the viewer's exposure to Ms.Gullory is cut short by a street thug, who slays her by the ice skating pond.  Mr.Pearce responds with an expressions of shock and agony.  Again, such expressions are within the capabilities of an rare and occasional trained actor, and not within the realm of amateur actors.  In response, Mr.Pearce devotes himself to building a time machine, with the goal of traveling back to the past to change what happened.  He succeeds, in part, because he is able to meet Ms.Guillory shortly before her demise, and he succeeds further, by convincing her to travel away from the ice skating pond.  But alas, she is run over in an accident with a horse-drawn carriage.      The film shifts gears, and Mr.Pearce decides to travel forwards in time for an answer, where his goal is still to save Ms.Guillory.  What thus occurs is, at least in my opinion, one of the finest sequences in film history.  The earlier film from 1960 contains an equivalent sequence, but it is clumsy and clunky compared to this sequence in the Guy Pearce movie.  At any rate, the viewer is treated to a particular, static location (Mr. Pearce's laboratory) but where time races forwards by many years.  We see a view of the women's shop across the street from the laboratory, where the style of the fashions displayed in the front window is updated every few seconds, where the hemlines rise ever higher.  We see a bird's eye view of the laboratory, where small structures are replaced by skyscrapers.  Eventually, Mr.Pearce halts his time travel, and finds himself in a modern society where the mining taking place on the moon resulted in an accident where the moon got partially shattered, and is missing a few chunks.      This moon scene is one of the cleverest in the history of cinema, in my opinion.  An engaging part of this movie, is when Mr.Pearce meets an ordinary young woman with her bicycle.  The woman glances at the time machine, parked in an alleyway, and she makes the comment that it looks like a cappuccino machine.  (I like cappuccinos and, to my knowledge, this is one of the few existing jokes about cappuccinos. To digress a bit, it might be noted that Gary Larson has a cartoon about lattes, where a cowboy says, \\"Latte, Jed?\\")  Then, Mr.Pearce leaves the bicycle woman, and continues traveling in time.  The viewer is treated to scenes where thousands of years are compressed into a minute, and we see erosion and canyons being formed with the passage of time.  Mr.Pearce eventually halts in the far, distant future (the year 802,701 A.D.), where he makes an acquaintance with the Eloi people and the lovely Samantha Mumba.  There are many engaging and charming episodes in this part of the movie, when Mr.Pearce gets to know the Eloi people, and where he wins their trust.      Much of the rest of the movie concerns the Morlocks and their evil leader, Jeremy Irons.  While I did not particularly care for the part of the movie featuring the Morlocks, nothing can be done about this, since they are an integral part of the original TIME MACHINE novel.  At any rate, the Morlocks are scary and they jump around like grasshoppers and they chase people, and eventually they eat people (but this eating is not shown). First of all, Mr.Irons just looks like himself but with tons of white makeup.  Aside from the gobs of white makeup, there was no attempt to change his morphology from that of a regular human being. B004Y1USV2|5.0|Fly From Here is rapidly becoming one of my favorite Yes albums of all time - certainly in the top 5. That comes as no surprise to me really considering that Drama is actually my favorite. Unlike what some people are calling this, I don't consider it Drama part 2. Calling it a natural progression from Drama is one thing, and it is legitimate. However, I feel that this release is basically the guys saying \\"Drama was not our most successful so let's try and bring back some of the classic sound\\". And that is exactly what Fly From Here sounds like to me......Drama era lineup sounding a bit more like the Classic Yes.  There is no denying that songs like Fly From Here part IV - Bumpy Ride sounds pre-Drama (although not by much).  Solitare also sounds nothing like anything from Drama - including White Car.    So, what about the title track, which takes up about half of the album? Well, it is certainly a fantastic song. I would have preferred that it didn't have distinct breaks as it takes away from the epic nature of the track(s) - something else that reminded me of classic Yes. I compared the `demos' from Buggles Adventures of Modern Recording to the versions on Fly From Here and while the melody is basically the same, you can see the influence of Yes all over it. That is a good thing.  Same thing, although to a greater extent, with Life on a Film Set which is basically Riding a Tide from Adventures of Modern Recording.    I'm also very impressed with The Man You Always Wanted Me to Be (co-written by Chris Squire with some outside help, including former Syn member Gerald Johnson). I love the fact that Chris sings lead vocals on it although not really surprising since this song is supposedly from aborted Chris Squire solo album sessions.    As far as Benoit David's performance, I think it is nothing short of excellent.  Not much else to say about that. I would just like to see him contribute more to the songwriting.    Probably the most disappointing thing about Fly From Here is the length.  47 minutes is almost unacceptable in this day and age, especially for a band that has not had any new music in ten years (another one my favorites - Magnification).  Only about 20 minutes is not a Horn/Downes composition and while that is not a bad thing, a lot of that is not even really \\"new\\".  I guess I would just expect more from Chris Squire and even Steve Howe after so long. I'm especially disappointed by this considering that there is an extended, or full, version of Hour of Need on the Japanese release.  And from the little excerpt I heard, it sounds fantastic.     While nothing to do with this release itself, I think mentioning Jon Anderson is warranted here.  I don't think anyone in their right mind would doubt that Jon is not only the voice of Yes but also a valuable songwriter. However, people really need to move on and get over the fact that Yes can exist without Jon. How is this any different than when an artist goes and makes a solo album while still in the band? Jon has exhibited nothing but sour grapes with anything Yes that has nothing to do with him. From calling this music `dated' to not performing songs like Tempus Fugit after rejoining Yes (perhaps he forgot that his longtime co-members of Yes wrote Tempus Fugit prior to Trevor Horn and Geoff Downes joining.

B004XISI4A|2.0|Reality can be harsh to happy endings. Evil is not always defeated, the hero does not always get the girl, and nobody ever really lives happily ever after. That's fantasy.<br /><br />Take, for example, the tale of a plucky fantasy author, battling to finish his life's work, who overcomes a six-year-long bout of writer's block to at last complete the fifth installment in his epic. It would be nice to think that the book thus produced was worth the wait. That would be the happy ending. But reality can be harsh to happy endings. \\"A Dance with Dragons\\" is not the book I waited six years to read, and to wish otherwise would be fantasy.<br /><br />George R. R. Martin's \\"A Song of Ice and Fire\\" series was never mere fantasy, but with each new volume, it is becoming more and more a daytime soap opera.<br /><br />Mr Martin famously spent the second half of the 80s working in television, and if I may jump to unwarranted conclusions, this experience seems to have helped him break new ground by infusing fantasy with some of the best aspects of TV--sharply-drawn, sympathetic characters, crisp, witty dialogue, and intricate plotting.<br /><br />He's also made clever use of catchphrases and personal mottoes to provide a kind of leitmotif to each character's story and give a sense of continuity and cohesion to the tale: \\"A Lannister always pays his debts\\", \\"If I look back, I am lost\\", \\"You know nothing, Jon Snow.\\" More famously, he's gotten good mileage from his willingness to kill off seemingly key characters at surprising moments.<br /><br />Maintaining such a high standard of writing for even one book would have been an impressive feat. Mr Martin managed it for three, stumbled on the fourth, and after 20-odd years working on the series, the fatigue is beginning to show.<br /><br />The personal catchphrases continue to get good airtime, with \\"a Lannister always pays his debts\\" featuring five times, \\"If I look back, I am lost (or doomed)\\" six times, and \\"You know nothing, Jon Snow\\" an impressive 13. However, much of the other writing has become simply repetitive and lazy. The phrase \\"words are wind\\" also pops up 13 times in various character's mouths, \\"much and more\\" (meaning \\"a lot\\") gets used as hefty 30 times, but this is pipped for the number one spot by \\"(s)he was not wrong\\", at a teeth-gritting 33 times. These phrases have become less a leitmotif, more a pianist banging the same three chords over and over again.<br /><br />As part of the series' gritty image, Mr Martin has never been shy about including sex in his stories, but now he appears to be shoehorning it in, simply for its own sake. One character spends the night before a siege having graphic sex. Another pays a surprise visit to one of his generals--and interrupts the latter mid-coitus. A description of a man being burned at the stake takes time out to tell us what happens when the fire reaches his genitals.<br /><br />The habit of killing off characters has likewise devolved into self-parody. Having already killed off most of the expendables, Mr Martin spends most of \\"A Dance with Dragons\\" only appearing to kill off characters, but not really. One is apparently executed, but isn't. Another appears to drown, but doesn't. Yet another seems to be beheaded, but--well, you get the idea. The effect is a bit like the boy who cried wolf, and cheapens the book's finale, in which two key characters appear to die, since by then the reader doesn't believe for minute Mr Martin will actually follow through.<br /><br />The other major drawback to the wholesale slaughter among named characters is that Mr Martin spends much time introducing a slate of new characters in much the same way that the old Star Trek series used to introduce new red-shirts.<br /><br />What is left? Plot, but not much of it. Most of the characters spend their time somnolently staggering from A to B. There are sporadic bouts of frenetic action, to be sure, but the story itself continues to plod along, fairly aimlesslyas far as I can see. With no resolution to any of the major plot lines anywhere in sight, it's increasingly hard to care about any of the latest crop of characters, knowing they probably won't live much longer than halfway through the next book (when and if it is ever published). It's only in the handful of chapters that \\"Dance with Dragons\\" shakes off its lethargy and wraps up in a number of cliffhanger endings.<br /><br />I would like to believe the series will get better, that all questions will be answered, all the plot lines will come together, but if there's one thing Mr Martin has taught me, it's not to believe in happy endings.as far as I can see. 0307477479|5.0|I'm surprised that this novel has received so many poor reviews, as I found it quite meaningful, as well as inventive. Perhaps it is because I am a baby boomer who has experienced some of the melancholy feelings associated with aging and watching old friends grow older and (not always) wiser. This brings to mind a question I had as I read this novel: How is it that someone who appears to be as young as the author, Jennifer Egan, has such a deep appreciation of time, both its good and not so good aspects? Perhaps Egan is older than she looks, but in any case I found myself identifying strongly with her characters and understanding what they were feeling as they moved through their lives.<br /><br />Admittedly, A Visit from the Goon Squad is not a linear narrative and it does take patience at times to keep reading until this roundabout plot makes sense. But that is how life is, and I suspect Egan intends to mirror reality. There are many things that happened in my life that I didn't understand until years later, after I had some distance on the event and was able to put it in perspective. And there were also things that I felt were hugely important at the time which, years later, don't seem to have any lasting impact.<br /><br />I love the music metaphor that Egan uses to frame her story, the various arcs of which remind me of dropping a needle at random on a 33 1/3 LP. You get a snippet of song that is part of the whole, but you may not realize that until you listen to the entire record. I know that this experience is specific to a time when music lived on vinyl and was played with a turntable, so the needle drop phenom is lost on readers who are not familiar with analog technologies. But that is the first thing I thought of when I got near the end of the novel and came across Alex from the book's first chapter, now grown and on his way to see Scotty in concert.  I like the way that Egan interweaves and overlaps the lives of her characters -- six degrees and all that.<br /><br />As for the 'goon squad' title, I love that as well. There is nothing so apt as equating the ravages of time to being worked over by a goon squad. And Egan makes that idea clear at several points in the book when the dialogue alludes to the \\"time is a goon\\" quote. I think that characterization is dead on.<br /><br />If you like straight up narrative with a clearly drawn plot line, then this novel is probably not for you. But if you are a bit adventuresome and a little bit queasy watching the sands slip through the hourglass, I suggest you give this book a try. Definitely not a game where you just randomly tap around on the screen without any intellectual input from yourself. There is an actual sense of accomplishment when a difficult level is solved, because this game gets difficult fairly quickly. When you are familiar enough with the game's controls, you can turn off the \\"tip bubbles\\" (which are distracting) from the main menu.<br /><br />When you solve a level, you can take a screenshot for future reference (as well as to brag to your equally obsessed Refraction-geek friend that you're smarter than him, and you have a screenshot to prove it!).<br /><br />The user interface is simple and uncluttered. Graphics are sharp and nicely laid-out - this is a very attractive game for those with an appreciation for symmetry, lines and geometric shapes. No annoying soundtrack playing in the background, which is a huge plus for me. Play this for a while, and you'll become an expert at determining what the component primary colors of many other colors are. Plus, you can switch between \\"pigment\\" and \\"light\\" color styles and see how the light beams interact with each other in two ways, not just one.<br /><br />Mr. Spock would like this game. And so will you, if you like games that require contemplation and problem-solving skills to conquer. With 120 levels, and more than one way to solve each level, \\"Refraction\\" will be on your phone for a long, long time.

B004HO6I2E|3.0|You've seen this movie on countless occasions; Freaky Friday, Face/Off, Freaky Friday again, The Skeleton Key, and Freaky Friday a third time amongst a half a dozen other movies I'm probably overlooking. Body swapping isn't exactly a new concept. Truth be told though, while the idea has been used in an R-rated film before it may be something new to the R-rated comedy genre. Maybe that's why we're taking this rather redundant feeling trip once again or maybe it's only to capitalize on Ryan Reynolds blossoming popularity and have long time fans of his reminisce about their first time watching Van Wilder. The Change-Up is coming whether you like it or not and while it isn't the funniest film of the year or even the most original, it isn't quite as bad as you might be expecting.<br /><br />Dave Lockwood (Jason Bateman) has been working hard his whole life. He thrived to make work the most important thing in his life and that shows as he's one of the best lawyers in town. Meanwhile Mitch Planko (Ryan Reynolds) has barely worked a day in his life and tends to quit anything when it shows the first signs of difficulty. Dave never seems to have time for anything while Mitch just wants someone to come home to and feel appreciated. So after a night at the bar and a conversation while urinating in a public fountain the two accidentally switch bodies.<br /><br />Raunchiness rears its ugly head not even five minutes into the movie. The Change-Up is probably the most obscene and easily the most vulgar movie of the year, at least thus far. The things that come out of Mitch Planko's mouth, whether he's in the body of Ryan Reynolds or Jason Bateman, walks a thin line between being profane and being downright offensive. It's so unbelievable that you can't help but laugh at times. The really bizarre thing is that I couldn't help but somewhat relate to the Mitch character. Not so much the sleeping around with countless women part, but his sense of humor, use of vulgarity, and dislike for children. That's basically me in a nutshell.<br /><br />Jason Bateman and Ryan Reynolds do a pretty flawless job of swapping each other's roles. The roles they both start off with are pretty much the roles we've always seen the two actors play, so seeing them switch it up a bit was kind of interesting. Jason Bateman will always bring a little bit of Michael Bluth with him wherever he goes while Reynolds will probably always be seen as Van Wilder first. That's part of their charm and part of the appeal for the fans that have been following them longer than a year or two. Bateman playing the douchebag is actually a lot of fun though and Reynolds is kind of charming in the nice guy role in an awkward and goofy kind of way. Their chemistry is really the thing that makes the comedy work as well as it does. Leslie Mann also has her moments, which usually come once her husband starts acting like his best friend. This is probably Olivia Wilde's best movie performance in the past few years though. She was so boring in both TRON: Legacy and Cowboys & Aliens. At least in this she gets to show that she's both professional and has a kinkier side while portraying much more emotion than in those two films.<br /><br />The movie progressively snowballs further and further into ridiculous territory as it drudges on though. The scene in the kitchen where Mitch is trying to figure out how to feed Dave's children just about ruined the entire thing for me and the whole Tatiana thing is really stretching it. Each character gets to coach the other while in the other's respective body and those scenes might have honestly been the best in the movie. They were concise and had all the strongest points of the movie in those segments. It's a shame the entire movie couldn't quite hold onto that throughout its duration. With all the fecal matter going into people's mouths, ball cupping, genital shaving, and women being shown on the toilet at inopportune moments, the toilet humor got to be a bitmuch at times and that's coming from a guy who's a fan of that type of humor.<br /><br />The Change-Up is slightly above the mediocre line for R-rated comedies this year. It's slightly similar to Hall Pass with a little bit of Bridesmaids and Your Highness thrown in for good measure. While it is fairly humorous at times, its outrageousness is its own downfall as certain bits are too ridiculous for its own good. However, The Change-Up was still a crowd pleaser and is sure to at least make a modest profit at the box office.from a guy who's a fan of that type of humor. B004K4AVAG|5.0|Lady Gaga's second album is noticeably different from the first, in mood and in thematic content. I was not in love with it on the first listen, but on the second listen, I began to see the light, and each successive listen has made me appreciate it more. I say this to make the point that anyone who gave up after one listen because it was not enough like \\"The Fame\\" is missing out on something special.<br /><br />It is a rather dark album; not all dark, but in my opinion the darker moments are some of the best ones. \\"Marry The Night\\" is mainstream dance music, which sounds innocent enough, but the lyrics say \\"I'm gonna marry the dark/Gonna make love to the stark/I'm a soldier to my own emptiness\\", so this is no ordinary night out. On \\"Government Hooker\\", the unique identity of this album really begins. It takes us into a sort of underworld, and to support this, it has a very nasty, I would say menacing, bass groove, with the voice of \\"Government\\" saying things like \\"Back up and turn around/Put your hands on the ground/Get down.\\" \\"Judas\\" is one of many songs which feature religious references. It has a rock/disco vibe, behind lyrics such as \\"Jesus is my virtue/And Judas is the demon I cling to.\\" \\"Americano\\" is a Hispanic sequel to \\"Alejandro\\" from \\"The Fame\\", but it is less playful, more desperate.  \\"Scheibe\\" adds German to Gaga's linguisic repertoire, and the music is rock/disco like that in \\"Judas\\", with more bass groove similar to that in \\"Government Hooker\\". The lyrics have a feminist edge: \\"When I'm on a mission/I rebuke my condition/If you're a strong female you don't need permission.\\"<br /><br />\\"Bloody Mary\\" is one of my favorites on the album. There are dark sounds and visions in \\"Heavy Metal Lover\\" and \\"Electric Chapel\\", two more of my favorites. In the first one, \\"Red wine and cheap perfume/And a filthy shout\\" accompany the night's amorous activities in which \\"A group does it better\\" in \\"a full house of leather\\". In \\"Electric Chapel\\", love is more intimate and even mystical, beginning with \\"My body is sanctuary/My blood is pure\\", and ending with \\"I need something sacred from you/Down on our knees we'll find a way/To make a pure love work in a dirty world.\\" The sound of both tracks is appropriately dark and serious, with plenty of electric guitar in \\"Electric Chapel\\".<br /><br />There are other highlights, not necessarily dark ones. \\"Born This Way\\" is a retro-sounding dance number directed toward those who feel like outcasts from mainstream society. Likewise, \\"Bad Kids\\" is both an affirmation and a defense for Gaga's younger fans. I'm not sure what \\"Highway Unicorn\\" is all about, but the lyrics are lots of fun, about a girl who is a \\"free soul burning roads with a flag in her bra.\\" \\"The Edge Of Glory\\" ends the album the same way it began, with an exuberant mainstream dance anthem. Overall, I doubt that this album will be the \\"monster\\" of commercial success that \\"The Fame\\" turned out to be, but I do believe that it will enhance Lady Gaga's reputation as a hitmaker and songwriter. The incorporation of  themes that are more complex and more \\"adult\\" shows a growth that would be missing if she had set out simply to duplicate her earlier success.e dark sounds and visions in \\"Heavy Metal Lover\\" and \\"Electric Chapel\\", two more of my favorites. B004Y1USV2|5.0|\\"There's a fine line between genius and insanity.  I have erased that line\\" - Oscar Levant    There's a reason for that quote, and it'll be apparent as you read through this review of Yes' fine release, \\"Fly From Here\\".   Hands down, this is he best release since 1994's \\"Talk\\" and the most progressive since \\"Drama\\" in 1980.   It's the first to feature new singer Benoit David (who does a superb job), and the return of one of my favorite keyboardists of all time, Geoff Downes (who also does an incredible job).  Coupled with THE Yes guitarist, Steve Howe (who truly makes this album special), and the solid playing of Chris Squire and Alan White, FFH is a joy to behold, because it's the first album I can truly sounds like the band I've loved since I was 12 (OK, I'll date myself here, that was in 1973).  This is no Close To The Edge (then again what is?  That's like expecting another Lark's Tongues in Aspic from King Crimson; those were once in a lifetime albums and their like shall never pass this way again).  This is no 90125 (clearly no arena rock anthems here), nor is it Drama (the heavy and in your face playing is not here).  It is rather, a combination of all of them to varying degrees.  It also has that unquantifiable sound that is Yes.       The true stars of this album are clearly Howe, BD, and Geoff Downes.  They did the right thing in getting Geoff back to play on this album, because in all reality he's the only keyboard player who could bring the old tunes back to life, breathe new life into the newer compositions, and create the atmospherics with beautiful work on the pads as well as lovely melody and leads.  He is truly incredible, and for my money he's the guy who's the glue that holds it all together.  He was born to play on this recording...period.  It's one of his masterpieces in his long recording career for sure.  BD FINALLY can step out of Jon's very long shadow and sing in his own key at his own pace.  Sure he can sound like Jon at times, but he alsohas his Trevor Horn moments, and those moments that are distinctively his.  My only gripe with the playing is the rather pedestrian drumming by White, and average bass lines by Squire.  By no means are these guys bad; they are serviceable at the moment and do a good job, but given their talents they could have done a hell of a lot better ESPECIALLY because the music is so damned good.    The longest epic that Yes has ever written, clocking in at around 23 minutes, is the title suite \\"Fly From Here\\".  While it is cohesive on one level as a whole musically, it can also be argued that it is comprised of several songs stitched together by a common theme conceptually rather than musically.  And you'd be right on both counts.  Geoff and Steve both manage to connect the various parts with nice segue playing of their own.  The odd meter intro section leads into the beautiful and soaring \\"We Can Fly\\" where BD's vocals shine.  There is some great playing by the entire band on this piece of the suite, but then it leads into the incredibly beautiful \\"Sad Night At The Airfield\\".  It has an almost longing, mournful, and sorrowful feel to it, but the lovely acoustic playing of Howe and nice bass work of Squire add to the feeling, making it a very ethereal piece.  This is followed by the sudden shift into the odd meter introduced at the start of the composition via the Overture, \\"Madman At The Screens\\", but a full band workout with vocals this time.  Almost as a coda inside the suite and before the end is \\"Bumpy Ride\\" which leads into the main theme reprise ending what is now one of my favorite long form Yes compositions.    Next up is a rather poppy tune by Squire , \\"The Man You always Wanted Me To Be\\"; a nice song on it's own, but it really doesn't belong on this album (at worst) and is in the wrong order (at best) on the album.  Perhaps Steve Howe's solo piece, \\"Solitaire\\" might have been better here, but it's absolutely PERFECT where it it.  It comes after the slightly adventurous \\"Life On A Filmset\\", with its interestingly eerie start, but then it quickly changes into this driving tune led by Geoff's beautiful keyboard work.   This song almost seems to have been a part of a suite itself, but on it's own is yet another moment you realize the Downes/Horn influence on this album (especially since most of the title suite was written by both of them).   \\"Hour Of Need\\" is a sweet little song on the Domestic release but it's a tiger and an almost completely different song at that on the Japanese import.  There's an extended percussion workout for Alan that starts the song off and then the band launches into a majestic piece led by Steve's driving guitar...all which leads us into the regular shortened version of this song.  It's a lovely song, and I can't help but think what Crosby, Stills, and Nash would do vocally with this song.  Nice playing by Steve and perfect percussion by guest Luis Conte (who played back on a couple of Asia albums in the John Payne era) provide the background.  Nice pads and atmospherics abound courtesy Geoff's keys, and the short little solo is what Oliver Wakeman leaves from the original sessions.  Then rather then end as on the Domestic release, there is another beautiful Steve Howe driven guitar solo coda that highlights just some of his magnificent playing on this album.  I prefer the long version of the song (having bought the Japanese SHM-CD), but the short version is just fine as well.  The album ends with the aptly named, \\"Into The Storm\\"; a fantastic band piece where all the members contribute beautifully and you can really see this one being a barn-burner in concert if they extend it all into solo-land.  But it's a great song and ends the album on a high note.    As far as the production goes, it's a Trevor Horn affair, which means crisp and clean production and that's the case here.  The former lead vocalist for the band back in the Drama days also provides some backing vocals throughout the album (I hear him a lot on the title suite) as well as being the mastermind behind the first Yes album in a DECADE.  That is a LONG time to wait, but it was well worth it as this is a fantastic disc.    Genius and insanity?  Indeed...because there is a bit of both styles of playing from all parties on here.  As I said, it's nice to finally hear Benoit David in his new element, and to have Geoff Downes back on board (in addition to his duties with Steve Howe in Asia) is a big plus.  Overall, I couldn't have hoped for better considering all the turmoil the band (and the world) has been through the past decade.  I highly recommend this album, and I'd urge you to spring for the Japanese SHM-CD pressing if you can.  You get the longer version of \\"Hour of Need\\" as a bonus track (in addition to the shorter version); what you also receive is magnificent sound quality above and beyond the call of duty.  It's simply magnificent on the home theater system, computer, iPod, iPhone...name it.  It's stellar sound in any format.  Highly recommended no matter what country of origin...it's a new Yes album and FINALLY I can listen to the music without quizzical looks as I did the past few Yes albums.    This one put a smile on my face...finally.  I hope this line-up stays together for a couple of more albums, because they certainly can work some magic when all the cylinders are clicking...and on \\"Fly From Here\\", they are.

1849025975|5.0|Having now gone through Homage to Catalonia, I don't see how one can properly appreciate 1984 without having also read this book. This account of Orwell's time fighting in the Spanish civil war in 1936-1937, written immediately after the event, contains two threads. One is about Orwell's time at the front, presented in his typically effective matter-of-fact manner. The second concerns the internecine struggles of the revolution in Barcelona, pitting anarchists, republicans, socialists, and communists against one another in the 'events of May' and in the purges that followed - purges of which Orwell almost became a physical victim. As the introduction by Julian Symons notes, Orwell would remain a man of the left. But after the communist party destroyed the POUM, whose militia Orwell had joined, he could no longer believe in communism as a system. As the party and the republican government that was under their control imprisoned and shot the 'Troskyist' POUM members, vilifying them as Fascist spies, so did Orwell's journey begin towards Animal Farm and 1984. This is the watershed that must be read to understand the end point.    Incidentally, Antony Beevor (The Spanish Civil War) confirms what Orwell wrote of the communist campaign of lies against the POUM. This was worth checking, since Orwell himself writes that he is not a neutral observer, and the foreign press wrote things up quite differently at the time, gobbling up the party's blatant defamations. Also of note is the oddly-chosen title, since Orwell ignores the role of Catalonian independentism, which historians tend to make more of as a political factor. But this was, as pierces through the page, the age of world-sweeping ideologies. To be read both for its pulsing action and for its insight into Orwell's ideological transformation.

B005CMSDLE|3.0|\\"Hanna\\" is a moderately entertaining movie, worth viewing just for the terrific acting by the trio of Cate Blanchett, Saoirse Ronan, and Eric Bana. But ultimately, I can't give it that high of a rating because it lacks a heart and soul, and despite being a two hour long movie, the many action sequences squeeze out scenes or plot points that would have let us understand or feel for the major characters.<br /><br />Saoirse Ronan reprises her ongoing roles as a young teenaged girl on a journey of discovery. She has played this part before, in \\"City of Ember\\", and \\"Lovely Bones\\" and is a terrific actress. Here in this movie, her hair and eyebrows and eyelashes have been bleached nearly white (she is normally a natural light brunette), giving her an ethereal quality, almost like a fairy or a wood elf. The difference in this movie is that her character is not a normal teenage girl, as we soon discover, and so she has this flat and emotionless affect through most of the movie.<br /><br />Cate Blanchett, what to say except that she is again wonderful, playing this fiercely evil CIA agent. Blanchett has been one of my favorite actresses after her role as Galadriel - I couldn't imagine anybody else playing that role ever again.<br /><br />Eric Bana has never been one of my favorite actors, as he seems to have cornered the market on roles as earnest but ultimately failed heroes. But he is an excellent actor.<br /><br />The movie has a lot of action sequences, with lots of running. The change from one scene to another is herky jerky, as we jump from the wilderness of Finland to an underground fortress in Morocco, to a tourist campground in Morocco, to Berlin, and then once in Berlin, from a rundown amusement park to her father's apartment, to her grandmother's apartment and then back to the amusement park.<br /><br />The action is also excessively stylish as if it were supposed to mean something. Why have the final denouement in an amusement park, starting in a house styled like a gingerbread fairy tale house? Why have Cate Blanchett's #1 evil henchman whistle a jaunty tune as he stalks his prey? I mean, won't that give him away?<br /><br />The movie left tons of loose ends and unanswered questions at the end. The motivations of the two adult antagonists are left only partially answered. Just why was Cate Blanchett's character chasing so hard after her quarry? And why did she do all those awful things in the past? The only answer given by Eric Bana's character on his motivations for what he did is wierdly simple and unsatisfying: \\"Because kids grow up\\" (like duh, but don't you think a good father would want his beloved daughter to have something better in life than become a pre-programmed assassin?).<br /><br />At the end of the movie, I could say only that this movie was entertaining but not memorable. Like Saoirse Ronan's character, the movie was so busy running that all the heart and soul and motivations got left out.ve Cate Blanchett's #1 evil henchman whistle a jaunty tune as he stalks his prey? I mean, won't that give him away?    The movie left tons of loose ends and unanswered questions at the end. The motivations of the two adult antagonists are left only partially answered. Just why was Cate Blanchett's character chasing so hard after her quarry? And why did she do all those awful things in the past? The only answer given by Eric Bana's character on his motivations for what he did is wierdly simple and unsatisfying: \\"Because kids grow up\\" (like duh, but don't you think a good father would want his beloved daughter to have something better in life than become a pre-programmed assassin?).    At the end of the movie, I could say only that this movie was entertaining but not memorable. Like Saoirse Ronan's character, the movie was so busy running that all the heart and soul and motivations got left out.
